<div id="review_1178653887" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1178653887" />
    <a title="Nicola" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8803703-nicola"><img alt="Nicola" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587222161p2/8803703.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1178653887?book_show_action=true">Jan 24, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicola" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicola" href="/user/show/8803703-nicola">Nicola</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1178653887" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13740486058767614866">I was really pleasantly surprised by this book. I've been hearing a lot about it and on my quest to read more UKYA (since it's where I'm from and I want to support the UKYA scene) I decided to pick it up. I am so happy that I did as it is one of those stories that I completely devoured. <br/><br/>David is fourteen years old and has known since he was a child that he is really a female stuck inside a male body. His two best friends are the only ones to know and he is struggling to find the right way to tel</span>
  <span id="freeText13740486058767614866" style="display:none">I was really pleasantly surprised by this book. I've been hearing a lot about it and on my quest to read more UKYA (since it's where I'm from and I want to support the UKYA scene) I decided to pick it up. I am so happy that I did as it is one of those stories that I completely devoured. <br /><br />David is fourteen years old and has known since he was a child that he is really a female stuck inside a male body. His two best friends are the only ones to know and he is struggling to find the right way to tell his parents. He has already been branded the "freak show" at school and certainly doesn't want his life to become even more difficult. But he knows that he needs to become who he really is. <br /><br />Leo is the new kid school and his attempt to stay as invisible as possible fails when he steps in and stands up for David in a fight. David is insistent in becoming friends with him but Leo moved schools for a reason and David is set to become the only person to know the real truth of why.<br /><br />The characters in this book are wonderful. There are alternating points of view from David and Leo and each had a very distinct voice. Both of their stories are very moving in their own way and have the right balance of sensitivity, development and humour. <br /><br />One of the things I liked most about this story is that it's not <i>just</i> about being transgender. It tackles diversity, the class system, bullying, becoming a teenager and turbulent family relationships. Each theme is so beautifully written and with just the right amount of humour that you can be in tears one minute whilst laughing the next. <br /><br /><i>The Art of Being Normal</i> is an emotional and realistic coming-of-age story which challenges the idea of what being "normal" actually means. I highly recommend it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13740486058767614866" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1089048708" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1089048708" />
    <a title="Jess" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26551953-jess"><img alt="Jess" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1401578796p2/26551953.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1089048708?book_show_action=true">Oct 25, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jess" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jess" href="/user/show/26551953-jess">Jess</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1089048708" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15622088921655840313">It's high time the world of YA fiction had a real contender in the T corner of the LGBT spectrum, and <b>Lisa Williamson</b> stands up to the plate magnificently.<br/><br/>'<i>The Art Of Being Normal</i>' follows David, who stumbles through the minefield of high school and puberty knowing in his heart that everything is wrong... because he wants to be a girl, knows he is supposed to be girl. Navigating bullies gets a little easier for him though with a well aimed punch from Leo Denton, who struggles alongside David wit</span>
  <span id="freeText15622088921655840313" style="display:none">It's high time the world of YA fiction had a real contender in the T corner of the LGBT spectrum, and <b>Lisa Williamson</b> stands up to the plate magnificently.<br /><br />'<i>The Art Of Being Normal</i>' follows David, who stumbles through the minefield of high school and puberty knowing in his heart that everything is wrong... because he wants to be a girl, knows he is supposed to be girl. Navigating bullies gets a little easier for him though with a well aimed punch from Leo Denton, who struggles alongside David with a family that barely seem to notice him and a blossoming relationship with a beautiful girl that seems too good to be true.<br /><br />This book took me on a hike, up and down, making me laugh and tear up in turn. Both David and Leo are complex and delightful characters that suffer some teenage problems we can all sympathise with, and then some that we know little about, putting us in sometimes very uncomfortable shoes. This book offers up a glimpse of understanding, addressing gender and sexuality without flinching from the difficulties of the subjects addressed.<br /><br />It broke my heart and remade it and I feel like I need to re-read it and appreciate it all over again. An honestly wonderful must read for both teens and older readers. YA fiction needs more books like this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15622088921655840313" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1873324323" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16811298674568153756"><br/> This book is really important. There are so many people out there today struggling with gender identity and the desperate need to be looked at as "normal" because they do not fit into the specific and constricting constructs of society and it is just heartbreaking. Before this book, i did not have that much of an insight into transgender struggles. Even if this book is purely fiction, it still did open my eyes to some of the struggles in an honest simple prose imbued with complex depths. I real</span>
  <span id="freeText16811298674568153756" style="display:none"><br /> This book is really important. There are so many people out there today struggling with gender identity and the desperate need to be looked at as "normal" because they do not fit into the specific and constricting constructs of society and it is just heartbreaking. Before this book, i did not have that much of an insight into transgender struggles. Even if this book is purely fiction, it still did open my eyes to some of the struggles in an honest simple prose imbued with complex depths. I really do not have much to say except that i urge everyone who has not read it yet to read and be educated in any possible way and learn more even after reading in order to understand the struggles of the people going through this. It may not seem real to some of us, but it is for every transgender person out there who is not accepted. <br /> The only thing i did not like about the book was that it is written in the present tense because for some reason that i do not know, i just cannot stand books written in the present tense. I made it through though so yassss bihh. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br />So I have to read this for a class and I'm pretty curious about the book .</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13926728527081982357">This is one of those times I'm kicking myself for not even reading the book description before buying it. I saw a rainbow book for only $2 so of course I bought it on a whim without taking the time to read the back of the book.<br/><br/>If I had only read the description beforehand I would've immediately put it down.<br/><i>"David wants to be a girl."</i> <br/>That line says it all.   <span id="freeText13926728527081982357" style="display:none">This is one of those times I'm kicking myself for not even reading the book description before buying it. I saw a rainbow book for only $2 so of course I bought it on a whim without taking the time to read the back of the book.<br /><br />If I had only read the description beforehand I would've immediately put it down.<br /><i>"David wants to be a girl."</i> <br />That line says it all. It doesn't get better throughout the book either. The trans girl character is continuously referred to as just a boy who wants to be a girl, which isn't the case. Being trans isn't a matter of "wanting" to be another gender. This book constantly misgenders and doesn't properly talk about being trans. It clearly isn't written for trans kids, but I definitely wouldn't recommend it for people trying to familiarize themselves with trans issues either.</span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1138522920" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12142092139462212995">Lisa Williamson's <b>
  <i>The Art of Being Normal</i>
</b> is a moving, well-written reminder of how brutal, yet how beautiful, the world can be to those who are different.<br/><br/>David Piper has really never fit in. Apart from his two best friends, most of his fellow high school students ridicule him for being different. One of the school bullies has called him "Freak Show" since they were younger, but David is willing to wait him out until high school ends. His parents think he is gay, and are waiting for him to t</span>
  <span id="freeText12142092139462212995" style="display:none">Lisa Williamson's <b>
  <i>The Art of Being Normal</i>
</b> is a moving, well-written reminder of how brutal, yet how beautiful, the world can be to those who are different.<br /><br />David Piper has really never fit in. Apart from his two best friends, most of his fellow high school students ridicule him for being different. One of the school bullies has called him "Freak Show" since they were younger, but David is willing to wait him out until high school ends. His parents think he is gay, and are waiting for him to tell them.<br /><br />What David wants, more than anything, is to be a girl. But as he grows taller and more like his father, he wonders if this will ever be a possibility.<br /><br />Leo Denton is the new kid in David's high school, coming from a poorer area to the more posh private school. Overly exaggerated tales of his exploits at his last high school follow him, but he lets people say what they want about him. Yet while he wants to remain under the radar, two events occur which ensure that wish isn't granted: he stands up for David when he is being bullied, and then he falls for one of the most beautiful and talented girls in school. It's not long before secrets he hoped wouldn't be exposed come to light.<br /><br />I felt <b>
  <i>The Art of Being Normal</i>
</b> so accurately captured the feelings one experiences when you are different, when you are bullied, and how you just wish you could hide to avoid the ridicule and abuse. Williamson created such complex characters that you feel for and root for, characters you think about after the book is over. Even if once the story hits its stride you have a feeling how the plot will unfold, you're completely drawn into the characters' lives and you want to know what is going to happen.<br /><br />Like so many YA books out there these days, this type of book didn't exist when I was growing up. I'm so glad that it exists now, however, and hope that people read it, are moved by it, and perhaps convinced to change their behavior, to understand that their definition of "normal" isn't everyone's. So well done.<br /><br />See all of my reviews at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://itseithersadnessoreuphoria.blogspot.com">http://itseithersadnessoreuphoria.blo...</a>.</span>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1076889835" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1076889835" />
    <a title="Becky (Blogs of a Bookaholic)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8613391-becky-blogs-of-a-bookaholic"><img alt="Becky (Blogs of a Bookaholic)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1529956761p2/8613391.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1076889835?book_show_action=true">Oct 10, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Becky (Blogs of a Bookaholic)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Becky (Blogs of a Bookaholic)" href="/user/show/8613391-becky-blogs-of-a-bookaholic">Becky (Blogs of a Bookaholic)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">12+ who enjoy books exploring friendships, bullying &amp; gender identity.</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8613391-becky-blogs-of-a-bookaholic?shelf=ya">ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8613391-becky-blogs-of-a-bookaholic?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8613391-becky-blogs-of-a-bookaholic?shelf=mental-health">mental-health</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8613391-becky-blogs-of-a-bookaholic?shelf=subverting-stereotypes">subverting-stereotypes</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8613391-becky-blogs-of-a-bookaholic?shelf=diversity">diversity</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1076889835" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7980049521473967178"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1404251280i/10214586.png' width='400' height='171' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>This review was originally published on <a href='http://beckysblogs.wordpress.com/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Blogs of a Bookaholic</a>.<br/><br/><b>An amazing step forward in diversity in publishing, not such a step forward in engrossing storytelling.</b><br/>As both a reader and a psychology student, I’m always on the lookout for YA books that tackle serious topics and incorporate them into stories, whether that’s mental health, rare disorders or, in this case, gender identity. As a student, I was lucky enough to cover gender as a topic and it really opened my eyes, so I was excited to d</span>
  <span id="freeText7980049521473967178" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1404251280i/10214586.png" width="400" height="171" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>This review was originally published on <a href="http://beckysblogs.wordpress.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">Blogs of a Bookaholic</a>.<br><br><b>An amazing step forward in diversity in publishing, not such a step forward in engrossing storytelling.</b><br>As both a reader and a psychology student, I’m always on the lookout for YA books that tackle serious topics and incorporate them into stories, whether that’s mental health, rare disorders or, in this case, gender identity. As a student, I was lucky enough to cover gender as a topic and it really opened my eyes, so I was excited to discover a novel about a transgender teen was being published this year! I couldn’t wait to see how such a complex topic would be handled.<br><br>Like many books being released lately, <i>The Art of Being Normal</i> uses a dual narrative technique, switching between the point of views of David and Leo. It reminded me a lot of <i>Non Pratt’s</i> novel, <i>Trouble</i>, in the sense that the point of view switches were snappy and seamless and both voices felt unique. I have to say I preferred Leo’s voice to David’s which was a bit of an issue for me, and there was nothing particularly unique or memorable about <i>Williamson’s</i> writing style for me to grab on to, but despite this, I still found myself consuming this novel at a rapid pace and finished it in just three days!<br><br>I really liked how the plot of <i>The Art of Being Normal</i> explored what it would be like to be transgender and the issues involved. David is sure that he wants to be a girl and has known this since he was 8. He religiously keeps track of his body changing in a notebook, experiencing mounting dismay each time he goes up a shoe size or becomes taller. To him, these are signs that his body is betraying him, puberty is turning him into something he does not want to be, yet he cannot bring himself to tell his parents to do something about it. To resent and hate your own body, to be constantly acting out a role when you don’t know your lines and the battle of trying to be ‘normal’ vs accepting who you truly are must be such a difficult thing to deal with. How do you find a romantic partner that accepts you? How do you avoid being bullied by peers? How do you go about living your life in a way that makes you feel happy?<br><br>Yet while I was impressed by <i>Williamson’s</i> discussion of this topic, I couldn’t help but want <b>more</b>. I guess I was hoping this novel would go into some of the darker and more serious issues such as the mental health problems (like increased depression and suicide rates) and extreme prejudice transgender people face. While there were a few scenes involving bullying, none of these emotionally impacted me or led me to feel horrified, and in general events seemed to go a bit too smoothly.<br><br>I also felt the plot of <i>The Art of Being Normal</i> was thin. Despite what I’ve said above about wanting this novel to delve into transgender issues further, it was also important for me that this book was able to stand in its own right, that it would be a good story <b>with</b> diverse characters and important issues as a bonus, and I don’t think <i>Williamson</i> achieved this. For the first half of this novel barely anything happened, and I’ll be honest, I was bored. Then the second half of the book added in a random left-field <i>The Fault in Our Stars</i> type road trip mission to search for Leo’s estranged father, which felt forced to me, as if it had been stuck in because the book needed a point of conflict. I was always expecting this novel to be a character driven with David and Leo’s friendship acting as the glue that held the book together, but unfortunately even this fell short for me and I never warmed to their friendship, making the lack of plot all the more apparent.<br><br>Even with the average writing and the oddly paced plot, this wouldn’t have bothered me so much if I had really connected to or become invested in the characters. Unfortunately, this didn’t happen either. I quite liked Leo and how much of an enigma he was, as one of the only students at Eden Park School from the bad part of town, with a dysfunctional family and some serious anger management issues, he had a sort of appealing roguish personality and his mysterious past drew me it. The other main protagonist David, however, felt flat, unmemorable and passive and didn’t exude any kind of personality at all which was frustrating, because he was the main reason I picked up the book! I feel like apart from being transgender, I learnt absolutely nothing about David except for one vague allusion to him liking fashion, and subsequently, he felt one dimensional and I didn’t warm to his character. <br><br>Overall, although the characters, writing and plot of <i>The Art of Being Normal</i> were distinctly average for me, I did ultimately enjoy this novel and whizzed through it quickly, and I still want to champion it for its diversity and bravery for breaking the mold. I really hope more books like this will be published in the future!</span>
  <a data-text-id="7980049521473967178" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1144350117" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1144350117" />
    <a title="Lee Farnell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2106908-lee-farnell"><img alt="Lee Farnell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1467726308p2/2106908.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1144350117?book_show_action=true">Dec 28, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lee Farnell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lee Farnell" href="/user/show/2106908-lee-farnell">Lee Farnell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1144350117" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12986095067979010362">When I first picked up this book I had really high hopes for it. The idea of a young transgender protagonist was really appealing and (as fickle as this may be) I love the cover. It's what drew me in. <br/><br/>Having just finished the book, my overall thoughts are good, but not great. <br/><br/>Story wise I really enjoyed it. I think the overall story was great. Both David and Leo are wonderful characters and I loved their inner monologues of their struggle with being transgender; they were thoughtful and they wer</span>
  <span id="freeText12986095067979010362" style="display:none">When I first picked up this book I had really high hopes for it. The idea of a young transgender protagonist was really appealing and (as fickle as this may be) I love the cover. It's what drew me in. <br /><br />Having just finished the book, my overall thoughts are good, but not great. <br /><br />Story wise I really enjoyed it. I think the overall story was great. Both David and Leo are wonderful characters and I loved their inner monologues of their struggle with being transgender; they were thoughtful and they were real. I know when I was a teenager, I certainly had similar feelings with certain aspects of myself and I thought Lisa did a wonderful job of capturing those moments. I also thought the two had a great dynamic together, though I would of liked to see their friendship blossom a little more before they shared there stories with one another and set off on the journey to Kent. That section felt a little unreal to me, I feel like teenagers don't crack so easily to one another. <br /><br />I also would of liked to know a little more about Leo's mother and 'her side of the story' when it came down to Leo's father and what happened. I would of liked to see how it changed the dynamic between Leo and his mother. Apart from that, I thought the characters all played their parts well, especially David's mother who I thought was particularly well written. <br /><br />One of my only major vices with the book is that it started by jumping straight in with David proclaiming 'I want to be a girl.' but after that, it diverted and the beginning of the book mainly focussed on Leo and his growing relationship in the new school and I felt like Davids story became a little sidelined. Though we went through about his notebook and his secrets in his room, I felt like we didn't really get to know him until around halfway through the book when he opened up to Leo. At that point, I felt he opened up to us as well. I also wasn't really sure what the book was going to be about until it Leo's secret was also revealed. It was at that point when I became really invested. <br /><br />Overall, I think the book was a good read. It thought the protagonists were rather refreshing and I think the story, though it starts slow, is a good read once you know all aspects of both the lead characters.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12986095067979010362" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1134080614" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1134080614" />
    <a title="Lucy Powrie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7733998-lucy-powrie"><img alt="Lucy Powrie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1532296359p2/7733998.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1134080614?book_show_action=true">Dec 17, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lucy Powrie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lucy Powrie" href="/user/show/7733998-lucy-powrie">Lucy Powrie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7733998-lucy-powrie?shelf=ukya-2014-read">ukya-2014-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7733998-lucy-powrie?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7733998-lucy-powrie?shelf=own-books">own-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7733998-lucy-powrie?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7733998-lucy-powrie?shelf=to-review">to-review</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7733998-lucy-powrie?shelf=review-books">review-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7733998-lucy-powrie?shelf=arcs">arcs</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7733998-lucy-powrie?shelf=physical-review-copies">physical-review-copies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7733998-lucy-powrie?shelf=signed">signed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7733998-lucy-powrie?shelf=debut">debut</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1134080614" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5370882038812898759">The buzz around the release of The Art of Being Normal has been crazy! So many of my trusted friends and fellow reviewers have loved it so the pressure was on when I chose to read it myself.<br/><br/>Williamson has created a novel I wish had been written years ago. It’s incredibly brave – I don’t think there’s anything too similar to it published at the moment – and it stands out because of its themes and content. However, I’m sad because I wish there were more books just like The Art of Being Normal. I’m</span>
  <span id="freeText5370882038812898759" style="display:none">The buzz around the release of The Art of Being Normal has been crazy! So many of my trusted friends and fellow reviewers have loved it so the pressure was on when I chose to read it myself.<br /><br />Williamson has created a novel I wish had been written years ago. It’s incredibly brave – I don’t think there’s anything too similar to it published at the moment – and it stands out because of its themes and content. However, I’m sad because I wish there were more books just like The Art of Being Normal. I’m going to make it my mission to seek them out! I hope it leads the way to similar books being published.<br /><br />David has one wish: he wants to be a girl. It’s something that only his two best friends know. As he starts a new school year, Leo Denton starts in year eleven, a boy shrouded in mystery. He is determined to be invisible. The school year plays out and the two boys find out that things at Eden Park School don’t stay secret for long.<br /><br />I really loved both protagonists and especially how distinguishable they were. Each stood out on their own but also interacted well together, which is what I love in a multiple perspective novel.<br /><br />David was such a loveable character. Being the same age, I thought I could relate to him a lot and really sympathise with his situation. I wish he were my friend!<br /><br />Leo’s character development was amazing to read. It was almost as if it was unfolding in real time and I loved seeing him grow and change. Authors take note – this was a perfect character arc!<br /><br />Please, please, please read The Art of Being Normal! I want to scream from the rooftops about it, and I know I’m not the only one!</span>
  <a data-text-id="5370882038812898759" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('xSE6NAUJy+FM1vjSs5U45ZmlGjjdtpVfZcLLIh9BURnGLO7rCOz5hRhK8Ll5hGbkJ+lIqZcqbLqXWPd8pQ/RAw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Bt8Sz26H8ZHFIqK21KqClBoYLmYnswKN9BPO0tig0pMF0sYQY2LD9ZG+qt0eu9yVpFR8920v+2gGifKMYu5SiQ==')})
  }

<span id="freeTextContainer11530270560858233542">Lisa was born in Nottingham in 1980. She spent most of her childhood drawing, daydreaming and making up stories in her head (but never getting round to writing them down). As a teenager she was bitten by the acting bug and at 19 moved to London to study drama at university. <br/><br/>Following graduation, Lisa adopted the stage name of Lisa Cassidy and spent several happy and chaotic years occasionally gett</span>
  <span id="freeText11530270560858233542" style="display:none">Lisa was born in Nottingham in 1980. She spent most of her childhood drawing, daydreaming and making up stories in her head (but never getting round to writing them down). As a teenager she was bitten by the acting bug and at 19 moved to London to study drama at university. <br /><br />Following graduation, Lisa adopted the stage name of Lisa Cassidy and spent several happy and chaotic years occasionally getting paid to pretend to be other people. Between acting roles she worked as an office temp and started making up stories all over again, only this time she had a go at writing them down. <br /><br />Lisa lives in London, where she is lucky enough to split her time between writing and acting. In her spare time she reads a lot of books, continues to daydream and eats way too much ice cream. <br /></span>
